mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Blue Origin</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption people</div></th><td class="agent" style="line-height:1.35em;"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_(aerospace_engineer)" title="Robert Smith (aerospace engineer)">\nBob href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.5em;">Owner</th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">Jeff Bezos</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" id="cite_ref-reuters20180802_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuters20180802-2">[2]</a></sup>&#160;(2018)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">Subsidiaries</a></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li>Blue Origin Florida</li><li>Blue Origin Texas</li><li>Blue Origin International</li></ul></div><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right:0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height:1.35em;"><span class="url"><a class="external text" href="http://blueorigin.com" rel="nofollow">blueorigin<wbr/>.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Blue Origin, LLC</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Private_spaceflight" title="Private spaceflight">privately funded</a> <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_manufacturer" title="Aerospace manufacturer">aerospace manufacturer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> services company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Kent,_Washington" title="Kent, Washington">Kent, Washington</a>. Founded in 2000 by <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a>, the company is developing technologies to enable <a href="/wiki/Private_spaceflight" title="Private spaceflight">private human access to space</a> with the goal to dramatically lower costs and increase reliability. Blue Origin is employing an incremental approach from suborbital to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_flight" title="Orbital flight">orbital flight</a>, with each developmental step building on its prior work. The company motto is <i>Gradatim Ferociter</i>, Latin for "Step by Step, Ferociously".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motto_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-motto-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Blue Origin is developing a variety of technologies, with a focus on rocket-powered <a href="/wiki/VTVL" title="VTVL">vertical takeoff and vertical landing</a> (VTVL) vehicles for access to <a href="/wiki/Sub-orbital_spaceflight" title="Sub-orbital spaceflight">suborbital</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The company\'s name refers to the blue planet, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, as the point of origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motto_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-motto-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Initially focused on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital_spaceflight" title="Suborbital spaceflight">suborbital spaceflight</a>, the company has designed, built and flown multiple testbeds of its <i><a href="/wiki/New_Shepard" title="New Shepard">New Shepard</a></i> spacecraft at its <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_facilities" title="Blue Origin facilities">facilities</a> in <a href="/wiki/Culberson_County,_Texas" title="Culberson County, Texas">Culberson County, Texas</a>. Developmental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_testing" title="Flight testing">test flights</a> of the New Shepard,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> named after the first American in space <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a>, began in April 2015, and flight testing continued into 2018, with its first passenger-carrying spaceflight planned for 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\nOn nearly every one of the test flights since 2015, the uncrewed vehicle has reached a test altitude of more than 100&#160;km (330,000&#160;ft) and achieved a top speed of more than <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a>&#160;3 (3,675&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilometres_per_hour" title="Kilometres per hour">km/h</a>; 2,284&#160;<a href="/wiki/Miles_per_hour" title="Miles per hour">mph</a>), reaching space above the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="K&#225;rm&#225;n line">K&#225;rm&#225;n line</a>, with both the <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> <a href="/wiki/Booster_(rocketry)" title="Booster (rocketry)">booster</a> successfully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landing</a>, making reuse possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>  By 2016, the second <i>New Shepard</i> booster <a href="/wiki/Test_article_(aerospace)" title="Test article (aerospace)">test article</a> had made four flights, each time exceeding 100&#160;km (330,000&#160;ft) in altitude, before returning for successful soft landings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techcrunch20160402_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-techcrunch20160402-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge20160619_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge20160619-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Blue Origin has become a part of a "dramatic metamorphosis" of the space industry in recent years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vs201805_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-vs201805-10">[10]</a></sup> \nhaving moved into the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> spaceflight technology business in 2014, initially as a <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket engine</a> supplier for others via a contractual agreement to build a new large rocket engine, the <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a>, for major US launch system operator <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a> (ULA). ULA is also considering the <a href="/wiki/BE-3" title="BE-3">BE-3</a>, Blue Origin\'s smaller rocket engine used on <i>New Shepard</i>, for use in a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_stage" title="Second stage">second stage</a>&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Cryogenic_Evolved_Stage" title="Advanced Cryogenic Evolved Stage">Advanced Cryogenic Evolved Stage</a> (ACES)&#8212;which will become the primary upper stage for ULA\'s <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(rocket)" title="Vulcan (rocket)">Vulcan</a> <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> launch vehicle in the 2020s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20150413_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150413-11">[11]</a></sup>\nBy 2015, Blue Origin had announced plans to also manufacture and fly its own orbital <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_Space_Coast" title="Florida Space Coast">Florida Space Coast</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/New_Glenn" title="New Glenn">New Glenn</a>.  <span class="nowrap">BE-4</span> had been expected to complete engine qualification testing by late 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20180419_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20180419-12">[12]</a></sup> but the test program continued into 2019.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_(Spacecraft).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="1098" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_%28Spacecraft%29.jpg/220px-Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_%28Spacecraft%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_%28Spacecraft%29.jpg/330px-Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_%28Spacecraft%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_%28Spacecraft%29.jpg/440px-Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_%28Spacecraft%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_Incremental_Development_(Spacecraft).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue Origin\'s three vehicles as of late 2012: (1) Goddard subscale demonstrator, flight tested in 2006&#8211;2007, (2) early, subscale version of the suborbital New Shepard propulsion module (as flown in 2011; variations exist to the larger New Shepard that actually flew in 2015), (3) future Space Vehicle (on top of a future orbital launch vehicle which, when stacked, is Blue Origin\'s future Orbital Transportation System).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Blue Origin founder Jeff Bezos had been interested in space from an early age. A profile published in 2013 described a 1982 <i><a href="/wiki/Miami_Herald" title="Miami Herald">Miami Herald</a></i> interview Bezos gave after he was named valedictorian of his high school class. The 18-year-old Bezos said he wanted "to build space hotels, amusement parks and colonies for 2 million or 3 million people who would be in orbit.  \'The whole idea is to preserve the earth\' he told the newspaper&#160;...  The goal was to be able to evacuate humans. The planet would become a park."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp20130810_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-wp20130810-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1999, after watching the rocketry biopic film <i><a href="/wiki/October_Sky" title="October Sky">October Sky</a></i>, Bezos discussed with science-fiction author <a href="/wiki/Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson">Neal Stephenson</a> the idea of forming a space company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSR-2018-03-26_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-TSR-2018-03-26-16">[16]</a></sup> Blue Origin was founded in 2000 in <a href="/wiki/Kent,_Washington" title="Kent, Washington">Kent, Washington</a>, and began developing both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_propulsion" title="Rocket propulsion">rocket propulsion</a> systems and <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sir20170414_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-sir20170414-17">[17]</a></sup>\nSince the founding, the company was very secretive about its plans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msnbc20060624_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc20060624-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsj20131207_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsj20131207-19">[19]</a></sup> and emerged from its "self-imposed silence" only after 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sir20170414_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-sir20170414-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the company was formally incorporated in 2000, its existence became public only in 2003, when Bezos began buying land in Texas, and interested parties followed up on the purchases. This was a topic of some interest in local politics, and Bezos\' rapid aggregation of lots under a variety of whimsically named shell companies was called a "land grab".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 2003 to 2017, Blue Origin was led by its president, <a href="/wiki/Rob_Meyerson" title="Rob Meyerson">Rob Meyerson</a>. Now Senior Vice President, Meyerson leads the Advanced Development Program business.\n</p><p>In July 2013, the company employed approximately 250 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw20160305_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-gw20160305-21">[21]</a></sup> By May 2015, they had grown to approximately 400 employees, with 350 of those working on engineering, manufacturing and business operations in the Kent location<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-st20150531_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-st20150531-22">[22]</a></sup> and approximately 50 in Texas supporting the engine-test and suborbital test-flight facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-st20150531_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-st20150531-22">[22]</a></sup>\nBy April 2017, the company had more than 1000 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sir20170414_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-sir20170414-17">[17]</a></sup>  In August 2018, the company was more than 1500 employees, more than double the number in early 2016, and is "expected to double again by the time New Glenn is flying."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters20180802_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-reuters20180802-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of 2016, Blue Origin was spending US$1 billion a year, funded by Jeff Bezos\' sales of Amazon stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-2017-04-05_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-2017-04-05-23">[23]</a></sup> In both 2017, and again in 2018, Bezos made public statements that he intends to fund Blue Origin with US$1 billion per year from sales of his equity in Amazon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bi20180428_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-bi20180428-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Shepard">New Shepard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: New Shepard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Shepard" title="New Shepard">New Shepard</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg/220px-Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg/330px-Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg/440px-Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_updated_logo_2015.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Company logo, 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>As early as 2005, Bezos had discussed plans to create a vertical-takeoff and landing spaceship called <a href="/wiki/New_Shepard" title="New Shepard">New Shepard</a>. Plans for New Shepard were initially kept quiet, but Blue Origin\'s website indicated Bezos\' desire to, "lower the cost of spaceflight so that ... we humans can better continue exploring the solar system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msnbc20060624_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc20060624-18">[18]</a></sup> By 2008, a publicized timetable for New Shepard indicated that Blue Origin intended to fly unmanned in 2011, and manned in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fg200812_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-fg200812-27">[27]</a></sup> In a 2011 interview, Bezos indicated that he founded Blue Origin to send customers into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a> by focusing on two objectives: to decrease the cost and to increase the safety of <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired20111113_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired20111113-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By July 2014, Bezos had invested over <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$500 million</span> of his own money into Blue Origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20140718_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140718-29">[29]</a></sup> The first developmental test flight of the <i>New Shepard</i> occurred on April 29, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The uncrewed vehicle flew to its planned test altitude of more than 93.5&#160;km (307,000&#160;ft) and achieved a top speed of <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a>&#160;3 (3,675&#160;km/h; 2,284&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senior_Staff_Writer_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Senior_Staff_Writer-31">[31]</a></sup> In July 2015, <a href="/wiki/NanoRacks" title="NanoRacks">NanoRacks</a>, a provider of services such as payload design and development, safety approvals, and integration, announced a partnership with Blue Origin to provide standardized payload accommodations for experiments flying on Blue Origin\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_New_Shepard" title="Blue Origin New Shepard"><i>New Shepard</i></a> suborbital vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> As of late 2016, Blue Origin projected that if all test flights operate as scheduled, they could begin flying passengers to space on the <i>New Shepard</i> in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2014, the company and <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a> (ULA) entered into a partnership whereby Blue Origin would produce a large rocket engine&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a>&#8212;for the <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(rocket)" title="Vulcan (rocket)">Vulcan</a>, the successor to the 10,000&#8211;19,000-kilogram-class (22,000&#8211;42,000&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> which has launched US national security payloads since the early 2000s, and will exit service in the late 2010s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sir20170414_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-sir20170414-17">[17]</a></sup>  The 2014 announcement added that Blue Origin had been working on the engine for three years prior to the public announcement, and that the first flight on the new rocket could occur as early as 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp201409_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-wp201409-34">[34]</a></sup>\nBy April 2017, <a href="/wiki/New_product_development" title="New product development">development</a> and test of the 2,400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a> (550,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a>) BE-4 were progressing well and Blue Origin was expected to be selected for the ULA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vulcan_launch_vehicle" title="Vulcan launch vehicle">Vulcan</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sir20170414_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-sir20170414-17">[17]</a></sup> In April 2015, Blue Origin announced that it had completed <a href="/wiki/Acceptance_testing" title="Acceptance testing">acceptance testing</a> of the <a href="/wiki/BE-3" title="BE-3">BE-3</a> rocket engine that would power the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_New_Shepard" title="Blue Origin New Shepard">New Shepard</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> to be used for Blue Origin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital_spaceflight" title="Suborbital spaceflight">suborbital</a> flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsr20150413_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsr20150413-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On November 23, 2015, Blue Origin launched the New Shepard rocket to space for a second time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> to an altitude of 100.53&#160;km (329,839&#160;ft), and vertically landed the rocket booster less than 1.5 metres (5&#160;ft) from the center of the pad. The capsule descended to the ground under parachutes 11 minutes after blasting off and landed safely. This was the first time a suborbital booster had flown to space and returned to Earth. \n</p><p><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This flight validated the vehicle architecture and design. The ring fin shifted the center of pressure aft to help control reentry and descent; eight large drag brakes deployed and reduced the vehicle\'s terminal speed to 623&#160;km/h (387&#160;mph); hydraulically actuated fins steered the vehicle through 192&#160;km/h (119&#160;mph) high-altitude crosswinds to a location precisely aligned with and 1,500&#160;m (5,000&#160;ft) above the landing pad; then the highly throttleable BE-3 engine re-ignited to slow the booster as the landing gear deployed, and the vehicle descended the last 30&#160;m (100&#160;ft) at 7.1&#160;km/h (4.4&#160;mph) to touchdown on the pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\nOn January 22, 2016, Blue Origin re-flew the same New Shepard booster that launched and landed vertically in November 2015, demonstrating reuse. This time, New Shepard reached an apogee of 101.7&#160;km (333,582&#160;ft) before both capsule and booster returned to Earth for recovery and reuse.  On April 2, 2016, the same New Shepard booster again flew, now for a third time, reaching 103.4&#160;km (339,178&#160;ft), before again returning successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techcrunch20160402_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-techcrunch20160402-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2015, Blue Origin announced details of an unnamed planned <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> launch vehicle, indicating that the first stage would be powered by its <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a> rocket engine currently under development, while the second stage would be powered by its recently completed <a href="/wiki/BE-3" title="BE-3">BE-3</a> rocket engine. In addition, Blue Origin announced that it would both manufacture and launch the new rocket from the <a href="/wiki/Space_Coast" title="Space Coast">Florida Space Coast</a>.  No payload or gross launch weight was given. Bezos noted in interviews that this new launch vehicle would not <a href="/wiki/Space_launch_market_competition" title="Space launch market competition">compete</a> for US government <a href="/wiki/National_security_of_the_United_States" title="National security of the United States">national security</a> missions, leaving that market to <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a> and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20150915_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150915-40">[40]</a></sup> Science-fiction author <a href="/wiki/Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson">Neal Stephenson</a> worked part-time at Blue Origin into late 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and credited the company\'s employees for ideas and discussions leading to his 2015 novel, <a href="/wiki/Seveneves" title="Seveneves">Seveneves</a>.\n</p><p>In March 2016, Blue Origin invited journalists to see the inside of its <a href="/wiki/Kent,_Washington" title="Kent, Washington">Kent, Washington</a> headquarters and manufacturing facility for the first time. The company is planning for substantial growth in 2016 as it builds more crew capsules and propulsion modules for the <i>New Shepard</i> program and ramps up BE-4 engine builds to support full-scale development testing.  Employment is expected to grow to 1,000 in 2016 from 600 in February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20160308_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20160308-42">[42]</a></sup> \nBezos also articulated a long-term vision for humans in space, seeing the potential to move much heavy industry completely off-Earth, "leaving our planet zoned strictly for \'residential and light industrial\' use with an end state hundreds of years out "where millions of people would be living and working in space."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw20160305_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-gw20160305-21">[21]</a></sup>  By March 2016, manned test flights were planned to take place by 2017, with possible commercial service in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedde_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fedde-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also in March 2016, Bezos discussed his plans to offer <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourism</a> services to space.  Pointing out the "entertainment" aspect of the early "barnstormers" in really advancing aviation in the early days when such rides "were a big fraction of airplane flights in those early days," he sees space tourism playing a similar role:  advancing "space travel and rocket launches, through tourism and entertainment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft20160312_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft20160312-44">[44]</a></sup>\nOn the other hand, there are no current plans to pursue the niche market of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_military" title="US military">US military</a> launches; Bezos has said he is unsure where Blue Origin would add any value in that market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft20160312_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-ft20160312-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2018, Blue Origin indicated that while it continued to plan to fly initial internal passengers later in 2018, it would not be selling commercial tickets for New Shepard until 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20180621_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20180621-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Glenn">New Glenn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: New Glenn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Glenn" title="New Glenn">New Glenn</a></div>\n<p>On September 12, 2016, Blue announced that its orbital rocket would be named <i><a href="/wiki/New_Glenn" title="New Glenn">New Glenn</a></i> in honor of the first American astronaut to orbit the Earth, <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a>, and that the 7-meter-diameter (23&#160;ft) first stage will be powered by seven Blue Origin <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a> engines. The first stage is reusable and will <a href="/wiki/VTVL" title="VTVL">land vertically</a>, just like the <i>New Shepard</i> <a href="/wiki/Sub-orbital_spaceflight" title="Sub-orbital spaceflight">suborbital</a> launch vehicle that preceded it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20160912_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsf20160912-46">[46]</a></sup> At the time of the announcement of New Glenn, Bezos revealed that the next project beyond New Glenn would be <i>New Armstrong</i>, without detailing what that would be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes-2016-09-12_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-2016-09-12-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2017, Bezos announced that Blue Origin had acquired its first paying launch customer for orbital satellite launches. <a href="/wiki/Eutelsat" title="Eutelsat">Eutelsat</a> is expected to start launching TV satellites in 2022 on Blue Origin\'s <a href="/wiki/New_Glenn" title="New Glenn">New Glenn</a> orbital launch vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-2017-03-07_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-2017-03-07-48">[48]</a></sup> A day after announcing Eutelsat, Blue Origin introduced <a href="/wiki/OneWeb" title="OneWeb">OneWeb</a> as its second customer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In September 2017, Blue Origin closed a deal for New Glenn with its first Asian customer, <a href="/wiki/Mu_Space" title="Mu Space">Mu Space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>  The company, based in Thailand, plans to provide satellite-based broadband services and space travel in Asia-Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> On December 12, 2017, Blue Origin launched technology that could treat chest trauma in a space environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Facilities">Facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_facilities" title="Blue Origin facilities">Blue Origin facilities</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1762" data-file-width="2611" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg/220px-Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg/330px-Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg/440px-Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_Florida_facilities.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production facilities near the Kennedy Space Center, Florida</div></div></div>\n<p>Blue Origin has a development facility near <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, and an operational launch facility in <a href="/wiki/West_Texas" title="West Texas">West Texas</a>, where they also <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_test_facility" title="Rocket engine test facility">test rocket engines</a>. Blue Origin has continued to expand its Seattle-area office and rocket production facilities in 2016&#8212;purchasing an adjacent 11,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (120,000&#160;sq&#160;ft)-building<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psbj20161020_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-psbj20161020-53">[53]</a></sup>&#8212;and 2017, with permits filed to build a new 21,900&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (236,000&#160;sq&#160;ft) warehouse complex and an additional 9,560&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (102,900&#160;sq&#160;ft) of office space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw20170222_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-gw20170222-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Blue Origin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manufactures" title="Manufactures">manufactures</a> rocket engines, launch vehicles, and <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsules</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_state" title="Washington state">Washington</a>. Its largest engine&#160;&#8211; <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a>&#160;&#8211; will be produced at a new manufacturing facility in <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In 2017, Blue Origin established a manufacturing facility for launch vehicles in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> near where they will launch <a href="/wiki/New_Glenn" title="New Glenn">New Glenn</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a>, after initiating design and construction in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft20160312_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-ft20160312-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20160308_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn20160308-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_vehicles">Launch vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Launch vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Early.2C_low-altitude_flight_test_platforms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early,_low-altitude_flight_test_platforms">Early, low-altitude flight test platforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early, low-altitude flight test platforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Charon"><i>Charon</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Charon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Blue Origin\'s first flight test vehicle, called <i><a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a></i> after <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>\'s moon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> was powered by four vertically mounted <a href="/wiki/Armstrong_Siddeley_Viper" title="Armstrong Siddeley Viper">Rolls-Royce Viper Mk. 301</a> jet engines rather than rockets. The low-altitude vehicle was developed to test autonomous guidance and control technologies, and the processes that the company would use to develop its later rockets. <i>Charon</i> made its only test flight at Moses Lake, Washington on March 5, 2005. It flew to an altitude of 96&#160;m (316&#160;ft) before returning for a controlled landing near the liftoff point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Origin_Charon_Test_Vehicle_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Origin_Charon_Test_Vehicle-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Charon</i> is currently on display at The <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">Museum of Fligh</a>t in Seattle, Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Goddard"><i>Goddard</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Goddard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The next test vehicle, named the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_Goddard" title="Blue Origin Goddard"><i>Goddard</i></a> (also known as PM1), first flew on November 13, 2006. The flight was successful. A test flight for December 2 never launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> records, two further flights were performed by <i>Goddard</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Shepard_suborbital_system"><i>New Shepard</i> suborbital system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: New Shepard suborbital system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/New_Shepard" title="New Shepard">New Shepard</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="252" data-file-width="396" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ef/Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg/220px-Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ef/Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg/330px-Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/ef/Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_New_Shepard_Launch_april_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Shepard Launch on April 29, 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>Blue Origin\'s <i>New Shepard</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital" title="Suborbital">suborbital</a> <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> system is composed of two vehicles: a crew capsule accommodating three or more astronauts launched by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_propulsion_module" title="Blue Origin propulsion module">rocket booster</a>. The two vehicles lift off together and are designed to separate during flight. After separation, the booster is designed to return to Earth to perform a <a href="/wiki/VTVL" title="VTVL">vertical landing</a> while the crew capsule follows a separate trajectory, returning under parachutes for a land touchdown. Both vehicles are intended for recovery and re-use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Origin_&#8211;_About_Blue_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Origin_&#8211;_About_Blue-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>New Shepard</i> is controlled entirely by on-board computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In addition to flying astronauts, <i>New Shepard</i> is intended to provide frequent opportunities for researchers to fly experiments into suborbital space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="383" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/91/Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg/220px-Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/91/Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg/330px-Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/91/Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_Test_Landing_with_Parachutes_April_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Shepard landing with parachutes on April 29, 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>A Federal Aviation Administration <a href="/wiki/NOTAM" title="NOTAM">NOTAM</a> indicated that a flight test of an early suborbital test vehicle &#8211; PM2 &#8211; was scheduled for August 24, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>  The flight in west Texas failed when ground personnel lost contact and control of the vehicle.  Blue Origin released its analysis of the failure on September 2.  As the vehicle reached a speed of <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a>&#160;1.2 (1,470.1&#160;km/h; 913.4&#160;mph) and 14&#160;km (46,000&#160;ft) altitude, a "flight instability drove an <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">angle of attack</a> that triggered [the] <a href="/wiki/Range_Safety_and_Telemetry_System" title="Range Safety and Telemetry System">range safety system</a> to terminate thrust on the vehicle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bo20110902_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-bo20110902-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 19, 2012, Blue Origin conducted a successful <i>New Shepard</i> pad escape test at its West Texas launch site, firing its pusher escape motor and launching a full-scale crew capsule from a launch vehicle simulator. The crew capsule traveled to an altitude of 703&#160;m (2,307&#160;ft) under active thrust vector control before descending safely by parachute to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landing</a> 500&#160;m (1,630&#160;ft) downrange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bo20121022_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-bo20121022-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2015, Blue Origin announced its intent to begin <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_robot" title="Autonomous robot">autonomous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_testing" title="Flight testing">test flights</a> of <i>New Shepard</i> in 2015 as frequently as monthly.  Blue Origin expected "a series of dozens of flights over the extent of the test program [taking] a couple of years to complete".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsr20150413_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsr20150413-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On April 29, 2015, <i>New Shepard</i> made its first test flight. The uncrewed vehicle flew to its planned test altitude of more than 93.5&#160;km (307,000&#160;ft) and achieved a top speed of <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a>&#160;3 (3,675&#160;km/h; 2,284&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senior_Staff_Writer_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Senior_Staff_Writer-31">[31]</a></sup> The crew capsule separated from the booster before returning to Earth for a landing under parachutes.\n</p><p>On November 23, 2015, <i>New Shepard</i> made its second test flight, reaching 100.5&#160;km (330,000&#160;ft) altitude with successful recovery of both crew capsule and booster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-First-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-70">[70]</a></sup> The booster successfully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">performed</a> a powered <a href="/wiki/VTVL" title="VTVL">vertical landing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 22, 2016, Blue Origin re-flew the same New Shepard booster that launched and landed vertically in November 2015, demonstrating reuse. This time, New Shepard reached an apogee of 101.7&#160;km (333,582&#160;ft) before both capsule and booster returned to Earth for recovery and reuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1152" decoding="async" height="391" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg/220px-New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg/330px-New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg/440px-New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Shepard_booster_and_crew_capsule_after_retirement.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Shepard booster and crew capsule after retirement</div></div></div>\n<p>On April 2, 2016, the same New Shepard booster flew for a third time, reaching 103.4&#160;km (339,178&#160;ft), before returning successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 19, 2016, the same New Shepard booster again flew, now for a fourth time, again reaching over 100.6&#160;km (330,000&#160;ft), before again returning successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge20160619_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-verge20160619-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A fifth and final test flight of the second booster and test capsule took place in October 2016.\n</p><p>The first flight of the third booster took place in December 2017.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Glenn_orbital_launch_vehicle"><i>New Glenn</i> orbital launch vehicle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: New Glenn orbital launch vehicle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Glenn" title="New Glenn">New Glenn</a></div>\n<p>The <i>New Glenn</i> is a 7-metre (23&#160;ft)-diameter <a href="/wiki/Multistage_rocket" title="Multistage rocket">two stage</a> <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> that is expected to launch prior to 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20160912_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-nsf20160912-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The design work on the vehicle began in 2012. The high-level specifications for the vehicle were publicly announced in September 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20160912_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-nsf20160912-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first stage will be powered by seven <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a> engines, also designed and manufactured by Blue Origin. The first stage is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_vehicle" title="Reusable launch vehicle">reusable</a>, just like the <a href="/wiki/New_Shepard" title="New Shepard">New Shepard</a> suborbital launch vehicle that preceded it. The second stage is intended to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expendable_launch_vehicle" title="Expendable launch vehicle">expendable</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20160912_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-nsf20160912-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_launch_vehicle">Orbital launch vehicle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Orbital launch vehicle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Revealed in 2015, the Blue Origin <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> launch vehicle&#8212;which began to be referred to by the placeholder name of "<i>Very Big Brother</i>" in March 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw20160305_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-gw20160305-21">[21]</a></sup>\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars20160309_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars20160309-73">[73]</a></sup>&#8212;is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TSTO" title="TSTO">two-stage-to-orbit</a> <a href="/wiki/Liquid-propellant_rocket" title="Liquid-propellant rocket">liquid-propellant rocket</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20150915_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150915-40">[40]</a></sup>  The launcher is intended to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_vehicle" title="Reusable launch vehicle">reusable</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs20150915_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbs20150915-74">[74]</a></sup>\nIn January 2016, Blue Origin announced that they plan to announce details about the launch vehicle later in 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20160122_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsf20160122-75">[75]</a></sup>\nand a few details were released in March 2016 when Blue Origin indicated that the first orbital launch was expected, from the Florida launch facility, in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw20160305_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-gw20160305-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first stage is to be powered by Blue Origin\'s <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a> single-shaft oxygen-rich <a href="/wiki/Staged_combustion_cycle" title="Staged combustion cycle">staged combustion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfn150408_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfn150408-76">[76]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_methane" title="Liquid methane">liquid methane</a>/<a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> rocket engine while the second stage will be powered by the recently qualified <a href="/wiki/BE-3" title="BE-3">BE-3</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tap-off_cycle" title="Tap-off cycle">tap-off cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a>/<a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> rocket engine.  The number of engines powering each stage has not been released, nor has the payload or gross launch weight specifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20150915_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150915-40">[40]</a></sup>  No details were publicly released as of September 2015.\n</p><p>Blue Origin intends to launch the rocket from the historic <a href="/wiki/Spaceport_Florida_Launch_Complex_36" title="Spaceport Florida Launch Complex 36">Launch Complex 36</a>, and manufacture the rockets at a new facility on nearby land in <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Park" title="Exploration Park">Exploration Park</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Acceptance_testing" title="Acceptance testing">Acceptance testing</a> of the BE-4 engines will also be done in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs20150915_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-cbs20150915-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_subsystems_and_earlier_development_work">Orbital subsystems and earlier development work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Orbital subsystems and earlier development work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Blue Origin began developing systems for orbital human spacecraft prior to 2012. A reusable first-stage booster was projected to fly a suborbital trajectory, taking off vertically like the booster stage of a conventional multistage rocket. Following stage separation, the upper stage would continue to propel astronauts to orbit while the first-stage booster would descend to perform a powered vertical landing similar to the New Shepard suborbital Propulsion Module. The first-stage booster would be refueled and launched again, allowing improved reliability and lowering the cost of human access to space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Origin_&#8211;_About_Blue_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Origin_&#8211;_About_Blue-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The booster rocket was projected to loft Blue Origin\'s biconic Space Vehicle to orbit, carrying astronauts and supplies. After orbiting the Earth, the Space Vehicle will reenter Earth\'s atmosphere to land on land under parachutes, and then be reused on future missions to Earth orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Origin_&#8211;_About_Blue_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Origin_&#8211;_About_Blue-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Blue Origin successfully completed a System Requirements Review (SRR) of its orbital Space Vehicle in May 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Engine testing for the Reusable Booster System (RBS) vehicle began in 2012. A full-power test of the thrust chamber for Blue Origin BE-3 <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket engine</a> was conducted at a <a href="/wiki/John_C._Stennis_Space_Center" title="John C. Stennis Space Center">NASA test facility</a> in October 2012. The chamber successfully achieved full thrust of 100,000 pounds-force (about 440&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsw20121016_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsw20121016-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Test_flights">Test flights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Test flights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Early test
