id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>April 2, 2016</td>\n<td>New Shepard 2</td>\n<td>Successful sub-orbital flight and landing of a reused booster<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techcrunch20160402_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-techcrunch20160402-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>June 19, 2016</td>\n<td>New Shepard 2</td>\n<td>Successful sub-orbital flight and landing of a reused booster: The fourth launch and landing of the same rocket.  Blue Origin published a live webcast of the takeoff and landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geekwire201606_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-geekwire201606-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>October 5, 2016\n</td>\n<td>New Shepard 2\n</td>\n<td>Successful sub-orbital flight and landing of a reused booster: The fifth and final launch and landing of the same rocket (NS2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20161005_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20161005-88">[88]</a></sup>  The capsule separated when the rocket was at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Q" title="Max Q">max Q</a> to test the abort sequence in most extreme circumstances; unexpectedly, besides the capsule, the booster was also recovered despite the stress suffered at separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>December 12, 2017\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Shepard_3" title="New Shepard 3">New Shepard 3</a>\n</td>\n<td>Successful sub-orbital flight and landing. The first launch of NS3 and new Crew Capsule 2.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>April 29, 2018\n</td>\n<td>New Shepard 3\n</td>\n<td>Sub-orbital flight and landing, second launch of NS3.  First flight with a commercial cargo payload of suborbital experiments, and first flight to be licensed by the FAA with a standard commercial launch license rather than under an experimental permit. Capsule peaked at 105.9 kilometres (65.8&#160;mi) altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>July 18, 2018\n</td>\n<td>New Shepard 3\n</td>\n<td>Ninth flight overall, and third of NS3, included several customer payloads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Before reaching the apogee, the capsule engaged an abort sequence to test its stabilization modules, after which it reached 120 kilometres (393,000&#160;ft).\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rocket_engine_development">Rocket engine development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rocket engine development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="BE-1">BE-1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: BE-1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Blue Engine 1</i>, or <i>BE-1</i>, was the first rocket engine developed by Blue Origin and was used on the company\'s <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_Goddard" title="Blue Origin Goddard">Goddard</a> development vehicle. The <a href="/wiki/Pressure-fed_engine" title="Pressure-fed engine">pressure-fed</a> <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant</a> engine was powered by <a href="/wiki/High-test_peroxide" title="High-test peroxide">peroxide</a> and produced 9.8&#160;kN (2,200&#160;lbf) of thrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlueEngines_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlueEngines-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlueEngineChart_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlueEngineChart-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="BE-2">BE-2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: BE-2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Blue Engine 2</i>, or <i>BE-2</i>, was a <a href="/wiki/Turbopump" title="Turbopump">pump-fed</a> bipropellant engine burning <a href="/wiki/RP-1" title="RP-1">kerosene</a> and <a href="/wiki/High-test_peroxide" title="High-test peroxide">peroxide</a> which produced 140&#160;kN (31,000&#160;lbf) of thrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlueEngines_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlueEngines-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlueEngineChart_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlueEngineChart-95">[95]</a></sup> Five BE-2 engines powered Blue Origin\'s PM-2 development vehicle on two test flights in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="BE-3">BE-3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: BE-3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/BE-3" title="BE-3">BE-3</a></div>\n<p>Blue Origin publicly announced the development of the <i>Blue Engine 3</i>, or <b>BE-3</b>, in January 2013, but the engine had begun <a href="/wiki/New_product_development" title="New product development">development</a> in the early 2010s.  BE-3 is a new <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a>/<a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> (LH2/LOX) cryogenic engine that can produce 490&#160;kN (110,000&#160;lbf) of thrust at full power, and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_throttling" title="Rocket engine throttling">throttled down</a> to as low as 110&#160;kN (25,000&#160;lbf) for use in controlled <a href="/wiki/VTVL" title="VTVL">vertical landings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20131203_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa20131203-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early thrust chamber testing began at NASA Stennis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsj20130117_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsj20130117-98">[98]</a></sup>\nin 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20131203vid_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa20131203vid-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By late 2013, the BE-3 had been successfully tested on a full-duration suborbital burn, with simulated coast phases and engine relights, "demonstrating deep throttle, full power, long-duration and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_restart" title="Rocket engine restart">reliable restart</a> all in a single-test sequence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20131203_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-pa20131203-97">[97]</a></sup>\nNASA has released a video of the test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20131203vid_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-pa20131203vid-99">[99]</a></sup>\nAs of  December&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the engine had demonstrated more than 160 starts and 9,100 seconds (2.5&#160;h) of operation at Blue Origin\'s test facility near <a href="/wiki/Van_Horn,_Texas" title="Van Horn, Texas">Van Horn, Texas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20131203_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-pa20131203-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>BE-3 engine <a href="/wiki/Acceptance_testing" title="Acceptance testing">acceptance testing</a> was completed by April 2015 with 450 test firings of the engine and a cumulative run time of more than 30,000 seconds (8.3&#160;h). The BE-3 engine powers the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_New_Shepard" title="Blue Origin New Shepard">New Shepard</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> that is being used for Blue Origin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital_spaceflight" title="Suborbital spaceflight">suborbital</a> flights that began in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsr20150413_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-tsr20150413-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="BE-3U">BE-3U</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: BE-3U">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <b>BE-3U</b> engine is a modified BE-3 for use on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_stage" title="Upper stage">upper stages</a> of Blue Origin <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbital</a> <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicles</a>.  The engine will include a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_expansion" title="Rocket engine expansion">nozzle better optimized</a> for operation under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vacuum_(outer_space)" title="Vacuum (outer space)">vacuum</a> conditions as well as a number of other manufacturing differences since it is an expendable engine whereas the BE-3 is designed for reusability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsj20131207_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-nsj20131207-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="BE-4">BE-4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: BE-4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/BE-4" title="BE-4">BE-4</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine,_sn_103,_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="2880" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine%2C_sn_103%2C_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg/220px-Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine%2C_sn_103%2C_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine%2C_sn_103%2C_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg/330px-Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine%2C_sn_103%2C_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine%2C_sn_103%2C_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg/440px-Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine%2C_sn_103%2C_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Origin_BE-4_rocket_engine,_sn_103,_April_2018_--_LCH4_inlet_side_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>BE-4 rocket engine, on display at the 34th Space Symposium, April 2018. It was the first BE-4 to be hotfire tested, on 18 October 2017.</div></div></div>\n<p>Blue Origin began work on a new and much larger rocket engine in 2011.  The new engine, the <i>Blue Engine 4</i>, or <i>BE-4</i>, is a change for Blue Origin in that it is their first engine that will combust <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_methane" title="Liquid methane">liquid methane</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">propellants</a>.  The engine has been designed to produce 2,400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a> (550,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a>) of thrust, and was initially planned to be used exclusively on a Blue Origin proprietary launch vehicle.  Blue Origin did not announce the new engine to the public until September 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20140917_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140917-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late 2014, Blue Origin signed an agreement with <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a> (ULA) to co-develop the BE-4 engine, and to commit to use the new engine on an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V#Replacement_for_the_RD-180_engine" title="Atlas V">upgraded</a> <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> launch vehicle, replacing the single <a href="/wiki/RD-180" title="RD-180">RD-180</a> Russian-made engine. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_V_successor" title="Atlas V successor">new launch vehicle</a> will use two of the 2,400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a> (550,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a>) BE-4 engines on each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_stage_(rocketry)" title="First stage (rocketry)">first stage</a>.  The engine development program began in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp201409_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-wp201409-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20140917_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140917-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When announced in 2014, and still in March 2016, ULA expected the first flight of the new launch vehicle&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(rocket)" title="Vulcan (rocket)">Vulcan</a>&#8212;no earlier than 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20140917_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140917-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw20160305_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-gw20160305-21">[21]</a></sup>  As of March 2018, Blue intends to complete <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_testing" title="Rocket engine testing">engine qualification testing</a> by late 2018, but no date for the first launch event has been recently stated by ULA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20180419_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn20180419-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pusher_escape_motor">Pusher escape motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Pusher escape motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Blue Origin partnered with <a href="/wiki/Aerojet_Rocketdyne" title="Aerojet Rocketdyne">Aerojet Rocketdyne</a> to <a href="/wiki/New_product_development" title="New product development">develop</a> a pusher <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">launch escape system</a> for the New Shepard suborbital Crew Capsule. Aerojet Rocketdyne provides the Crew Capsule Escape <a href="/wiki/Solid-propellant_rocket" title="Solid-propellant rocket">Solid Rocket Motor</a> (CCE SRM) while the <a href="/wiki/Thrust_vectoring" title="Thrust vectoring">thrust vector control</a> system that steers the capsule during an abort is designed and manufactured by Blue Origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late 2012, Blue Origin performed a <a href="/wiki/Pad_abort_test" title="Pad abort test">pad abort test</a> of the escape system on a full-scale suborbital capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bo20121022_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-bo20121022-68">[68]</a></sup>\nFour years later in 2016, the escape system was successfully tested in-flight at the point of highest dynamic pressure as the vehicle reached <a href="/wiki/Transonic" title="Transonic">transonic</a> velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20161005_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn20161005-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>By July 2014, Jeff Bezos had invested over <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$500 million</span> into Blue Origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20140718_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-sn20140718-29">[29]</a></sup>\nEven by March 2016, the vast majority of funding to support <a href="/wiki/New_product_development" title="New product development">technology development</a> and operations at Blue Origin has come from Jeff Bezos\' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Market_capitalism" title="Market capitalism">private investment</a>, but Bezos had declined to publicly state the amount<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20160308_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-sn20160308-42">[42]</a></sup> prior to 2017 when an annual amount was stated publicly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20180405_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20180405-104">[104]</a></sup> Blue Origin has also completed work for NASA on several small development contracts, receiving total funding of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$25.7 million</span> by 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20131205a_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20131205a-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aw20110422_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-aw20110422-106">[106]</a></sup> \nAs of  April&#160;2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Bezos is selling approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1 billion</span> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a> stock each year to <a href="/wiki/Private_equity" title="Private equity">privately finance</a> Blue Origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20180405_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20180405-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaborations">Collaborations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Collaborations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="With_NASA">With NASA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: With NASA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Blue Origin has contracted to do work for NASA on several development efforts. The company was awarded <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$3.7 million</span> in funding in 2009 by NASA via a <a href="/wiki/Space_Act_Agreement" title="Space Act Agreement">Space Act Agreement</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20131205a_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20131205a-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20131205b_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20131205b-107">[107]</a></sup> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCDev1" title="CCDev1">first</a> <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew Development</a> (CCDev) program for development of concepts and technologies to support future human spaceflight operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>  NASA co-funded risk-mitigation activities related to ground testing of (1) an innovative &#8216;pusher&#8217; escape system, that lowers cost by being reusable and enhances safety by avoiding the jettison event of a traditional &#8216;tractor&#8217; Launch Escape System, and (2) an innovative composite pressure vessel cabin that both reduces weight and increases safety of astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20131205a_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20131205a-105">[105]</a></sup>  This was later revealed to be a part of a larger system, designed for a biconic capsule, that would be launched atop an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> On November 8, 2010, it was announced that Blue Origin had completed all milestones under its CCDev Space Act Agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2011, Blue Origin received a commitment from NASA for <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$22 million</span> of funding under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCDev2" title="CCDev2">CCDev phase 2 program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aw20110422_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-aw20110422-106">[106]</a></sup>  Milestones included (1) performing a Mission Concept Review (MCR) and System Requirements Review (SRR) on the orbital Space Vehicle, which utilizes a biconic shape to optimize its launch profile and atmospheric reentry, (2) further maturing the pusher escape system, including ground and flight tests, and (3) accelerating development of its BE-3 LOX/LH2 440&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a> (100,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a>) engine through full-scale thrust chamber testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, NASA\'s Commercial Crew Program released its follow-on CCiCap solicitation for the development of crew delivery to ISS by 2017.  Blue Origin did not submit a proposal for CCiCap, but is reportedly continuing work on its development program with private funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>\nBlue Origin had earlier attempted to lease a different part of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Coast" title="Space Coast">Space Coast</a>, when they submitted a bid in 2013 to lease <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39" title="Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39A</a> (LC39A) at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>&#8212;on land to the north of, and adjacent to, <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral AFS</a>&#8212;following NASA\'s decision to lease the unused complex out as part of a bid to reduce annual operation and maintenance costs.\nThe Blue Origin bid was for shared and non-exclusive use of the LC39A complex such that the launchpad was to have been able to interface with multiple <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">vehicles</a>, and costs for using the launch pad were to have been shared across multiple companies over the term of the lease.  One potential shared user in the Blue Origin notional plan was <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a>.\nA competing bid for <a href="/wiki/Private_spaceflight" title="Private spaceflight">commercial use</a> of the LC39A launch complex was submitted by <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a>, which submitted a bid for exclusive use of the launch complex to support their <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">crewed missions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-os20130818_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-os20130818-114">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2013&#8212;before completion of the bid period, and before any public announcement by NASA of the results of the process&#8212;Blue Origin filed a protest with the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S.</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Accounting_Office" title="General Accounting Office">General Accounting Office</a> (GAO) "over what it says is a plan by NASA to award an exclusive commercial lease to <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> for use of mothballed space shuttle launch pad 39A".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed"><span title="This sentence may contain perspectives and opinions without stating whose they are. (March 2018)">attribution needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20130910_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa20130910-115">[115]</a></sup>  NASA had planned to complete the bid award and have the pad transferred by October 1, 2013, but the protest "will delay any decision until the GAO reaches a decision, expected by mid-December".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20130910_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-pa20130910-115">[115]</a></sup>\nSpaceX said that they would be willing to support a multi-user arrangement for pad 39A.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sp20130921_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-sp20130921-116">[116]</a></sup>\nIn December 2013, the GAO denied the Blue Origin protest and sided with NASA, which argued that the solicitation contained no preference on the use of the facility as either multi-use or single-use. "The [solicitation] document merely [asked] bidders to explain their reasons for selecting one approach instead of the other and how they would manage the facility."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa20131212_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa20131212-117">[117]</a></sup>\nIn the event, NASA selected the SpaceX proposal in late 2013 and signed a 20-year lease contract for Launch Pad 39A to SpaceX in April 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft20140415_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft20140415-118">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="With_DARPA">With DARPA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: With DARPA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Blue Origin cooperated with <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> in Phase 1 of the <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">DARPA</a> <a href="/wiki/XS-1_(spacecraft)" title="XS-1 (spacecraft)">XS-1</a> spaceplane program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Launch_Alliance">United Launch Alliance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: United Launch Alliance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 2018, it was announced that Blue Origin\'s BE-4 engine was selected by United Launch Alliance to provide first-stage rocket engines for their latest booster design, the Vulcan rocket. The BE-4 engine is set to replace the Russian-build RD-180 powering ULA\'s Atlas 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Blue_Origin" title="Portal:Blue Origin">Blue Origin portal</a></li></ul>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Armadillo_Aerospace" title="Armadillo Aerospace">Armadillo Aerospace</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin_landing_platform_ship" title="Blue Origin landing platform ship">Blue Origin landing platform ship</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Interorbital_Systems" title="Interorbital Systems">Interorbital Systems</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kankoh-maru" title="Kankoh-maru">Kankoh-maru</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_X-33" title="Lockheed Martin X-33">Lockheed Martin X-33</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Lander_Challenge" title="Lunar Lander Challenge">Lunar Lander Challenge</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Masten_Space_Systems" title="Masten Space Systems">Masten Space Systems</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-X" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-X">McDonnell Douglas DC-X</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/NewSpace" title="NewSpace">NewSpace</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Quad_(rocket)" title="Quad (rocket)">Quad (rocket)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Reusable_Vehicle_Testing" title="Reusable Vehicle Testing">Reusable Vehicle Testing</a> program by <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">JAXA</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/SpaceX_reusable_launch_system_development_program" title="SpaceX reusable launch system development program">SpaceX reusable launch system development program</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/VentureStar" title="VentureStar">VentureStar</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Zarya_(spacecraft)" title="Zarya (spacecraft)">Zarya</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_Origin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
